    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com">
    <meta name="dc.description" content="High-performance and low-cost electrode material is crucial for the development of Na-ion batteries. Herein, a novel honeycomb-structured biochar with high specific area (labelled as HS X-C) was constructed through a simple and scalable method, using Xanthium sibiricum fruits as starting material with the assistance of NaCl template. The HS X-C shows a long-range-disordered but short-range-ordered microstructure. As an anode for Na-ion batteries, it exhibits an initial coulombic efficiency of about 86.5%, a rate capability of 161 mAh g−1 at 4.0 A g−1 and a cyclability of 3000 cycles at 2.0 A g−1. Moreover, the Na-ion full cell matched with the NVP/rGO cathode exhibits an average operating voltage of about 3.3 V and a high energy density of about 180 Wh kg−1(total). Kinetic studies indicate that the reaction rate of this anode is jointly controlled by diffusion and pseudocapacitance. This work not only greatly improves the sodium storage performance of the biochar from Xanthium sibiricum fruits, but also demonstrates the importance of the adopted synthesis techniques for the electrochemical performance of biochar.">

    <meta property="og:title" content="Facile synthesis of honeycomb-structured biochar with high specific surface and its electrochemical properties as anodes for Na-ion batteries - Ionics">
    <meta property="og:description" content="High-performance and low-cost electrode material is crucial for the development of Na-ion batteries. Herein, a novel honeycomb-structured biochar with high specific area (labelled as HS X-C) was constructed through a simple and scalable method, using Xanthium sibiricum fruits as starting material with the assistance of NaCl template. The HS X-C shows a long-range-disordered but short-range-ordered microstructure. As an anode for Na-ion batteries, it exhibits an initial coulombic efficiency of about 86.5%, a rate capability of 161 mAh g−1 at 4.0 A g−1 and a cyclability of 3000 cycles at 2.0 A g−1. Moreover, the Na-ion full cell matched with the NVP/rGO cathode exhibits an average operating voltage of about 3.3 V and a high energy density of about 180 Wh kg−1(total). Kinetic studies indicate that the reaction rate of this anode is jointly controlled by diffusion and pseudocapacitance. This work not only greatly improves the sodium storage performance of the biochar from Xanthium sibiricum fruits, but also demonstrates the importance of the adopted synthesis techniques for the electrochemical performance of biochar.">

    <script type="application/ld+json">{"mainEntity":{"headline":"Facile synthesis of honeycomb-structured biochar with high specific surface and its electrochemical properties as anodes for Na-ion batteries","description":"High-performance and low-cost electrode material is crucial for the development of Na-ion batteries. Herein, a novel honeycomb-structured biochar with high specific area (labelled as HS X-C) was constructed through a simple and scalable method, using Xanthium sibiricum fruits as starting material with the assistance of NaCl template. The HS X-C shows a long-range-disordered but short-range-ordered microstructure. As an anode for Na-ion batteries, it exhibits an initial coulombic efficiency of about 86.5%, a rate capability of 161 mAh g−1 at 4.0 A g−1 and a cyclability of 3000 cycles at 2.0 A g−1. Moreover, the Na-ion full cell matched with the NVP/rGO cathode exhibits an average operating voltage of about 3.3 V and a high energy density of about 180 Wh kg−1(total). Kinetic studies indicate that the reaction rate of this anode is jointly controlled by diffusion and pseudocapacitance. This work not only greatly improves the sodium storage performance of the biochar from Xanthium sibiricum fruits, but also demonstrates the importance of the adopted synthesis techniques for the electrochemical performance of biochar.","datePublished":"2023-10-04T00:00:00Z","dateModified":"2023-10-04T00:00:00Z","pageStart":"5195","pageEnd":"5203","sameAs":"https://doi.org/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0","keywords":["Na-ion     function refreshCartState(json) {
        var indicator = document.querySelector("#ecommerce-header-cart-icon-link .cart-info");

        if (indicator && json.itemCount) {
            indicator.style.display = 'block';

            var moreThanOneItem = json.itemCount > 1;
            indicator.setAttribute('title', "there ".concat(moreThanOneItem ? "are" : "is", " ").concat(json.itemCount, " item").concat(moreThanOneItem ? "s" : "", " in your cart"));
        }
        return json;
    }

                        <ul class="c-article-author-list c-article-author-list--short js-no-scroll" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"><li class="c-article-author-list__item"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Wei-Zhang-Aff1" data-author-popup="auth-Wei-Zhang-Aff1" data-author-search="Zhang, Wei" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" data-track-index="1_4">Wei Zhang</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1" tabindex="-1">1</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Mingqi-Li-Aff1-Aff2" data-author-popup="auth-Mingqi-Li-Aff1-Aff2" data-author-search="Li, Mingqi" data-corresp-id="c1" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" data-track-index="2_4">Mingqi Li<svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-mail-medium"></use></svg></a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1" tabindex="-1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff2" tabindex="-1">2</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Yiwen-Chen-Aff1" data-author-popup="auth-Yiwen-Chen-Aff1" data-author-search="Chen, Yiwen" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" data-track-index="3_4">Yiwen Chen</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1" tabindex="-1">1</a></sup> &amp;                     </header>
                </div>

                <div data-article-body="true" data-track-component="article body" class="c-article-body">
                    
                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>High-performance and low-cost electrode material is crucial for the development of Na-ion batteries. Herein, a novel honeycomb-structured biochar with high specific area (labelled as HS X-C) was constructed through a simple and scalable method, using Xanthium sibiricum fruits as starting material with the assistance of NaCl template. The HS X-C shows a long-range-disordered but short-range-ordered microstructure. As an anode for Na-ion batteries, it exhibits an initial coulombic efficiency of about 86.5%, a rate capability of 161 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 4.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> and a cyclability of 3000 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, the Na-ion full cell matched with the NVP/rGO cathode exhibits an average operating voltage of about 3.3 V and a high energy density of about 180 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup><sub>(total)</sub>. Kinetic studies indicate that the reaction rate of this anode is jointly controlled by diffusion and pseudocapacitance. This work not only greatly improves the sodium storage performance of the biochar from Xanthium sibiricum fruits, but also demonstrates the importance of the adopted synthesis techniques for the electrochemical performance of biochar.</p></div></div></section>

                                <div class="main-content">
                                    <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>As an important energy storage device, Li-ion batteries have been widely used in various mobile devices and smart grids because of high energy density, long cycle life and environmental friendliness. However, the limited and unevenly distributed lithium resources seriously influence their sustainable development. Due to similar energy storage mechanism to lithium-ion batteries and abundant sodium resources, Na-ion batteries are expected to replace Li-ion batteries in some areas without strict requirements for volume energy density [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wei Q, Chang X, Wang J, Huang T, Huang X, Yu J, Zheng H, Chen JH, Peng DL (2022) An ultrahigh-power mesocarbon microbeads|Na+-Diglyme|Na3V2 (PO4)3 sodium-ion battery. Adv Mater 34(6):2108304" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e457">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Li C, Cao K, Fan Y, Li Q, Zhang Y, Guo Z (2023) Kinetically well-matched porous framework dual carbon electrodes for high-performance sodium-ion hybrid capacitors. J Colloid Interface Sci 652:1356–1366" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e460">2</a>]. Currently, developing high-performance and low-cost electrode materials is the key to the commercialization of Na-ion batteries. According to the sodium storage mechanism, the anode materials studied can be roughly divided into insertion type [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Kim D, Li OL, Kang J (2021) Maximizing the rate capability of carbon-based anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. J Power Sources 481:228973" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e463">3</a>], conversion type [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiang Y, Wang Y, Ni J, Li L (2021) Molybdenum-based materials for sodium-ion batteries. InfoMat 3(4):339–352" href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e466">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dai S, Wang L, Shen Y, Wang M (2020) Bismuth selenide nanocrystalline array electrodes for high-performance sodium-ion batteries. Appl Mater Today 18:100455" href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e466_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cao K, Zheng R, Wang S, Shu J, Liu X, Liu H, Huang KJ, Jing QS, Jiao L (2020) Boosting coulombic efficiency of conversion-reaction anodes for potassium-ion batteries via confinement effect. Adv Funct Mater 30(52):2007712" href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e466_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pan M, Lu S, Li Y, Li C, Cao K, Fan Y (2023) Copper hydroxyphosphate Cu2(OH)PO4 as conversion-type anode material for lithium-ion batteries. Ionics 29(6):2209–2215" href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e466_3">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu H, He Y, Cao K, Wang S, Jiang Y, Liu X, Huang KJ, Jing QS, Jiao L (2021) Stimulating the reversibility of Sb2S3 anode for high-performance potassium-ion batteries. Small 17(10):2008133" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e469">8</a>] and alloy type [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cheng X, Shao R, Li D, Yang H, Wu Y, Wang B, Sun C, Jiang Y, Zhang Q, Yu Y (2021) A self-healing volume variation three-dimensional continuous bulk porous bismuth for ultrafast sodium storage. Adv Funct Mater 31(22):2011264" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e473">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang H, Hasa I, Passerini S (2018) Beyond insertion for Na-Ion batteries: nanostructured alloying and conversion anode materials. Adv Energy Mater 8(17):1702582" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e476">10</a>]. Despite of high specific capacity, conversion-type and alloy-type materials suffer from large volume variation during cycling, seriously hindering their practical applications [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Peng M, Shin K, Jiang L, Jin Y, Zeng K, Zhou X, Tang Y (2022) Alloy-type anodes for high-performance rechargeable batteries. Angew Chem Int Ed 61(33):e202206770" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e479">11</a>]. Hard carbon is considered the most promising anode material for Na-ion batteries due to its easy availability and relatively small volume expansion [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Jiang M, Sun N, Ali Soomro R, Xu B (2021) The recent progress of pitch-based carbon anodes in sodium-ion batteries. J Energy Chem 55:34–47" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e482">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zhao W, Hu X, Ci S, Chen J, Wang G, Xu Q, Wen Z (2019) N-doped carbon nanofibers with interweaved nanochannels for high-performance sodium-ion storage. Small 15(46):1904054" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e485">13</a>]. However, the challenges of using hard carbon as anodes for Na-ion batteries are its low reversible capacity, high working potentials and low initial coulombic efficiency, which seriously sacrifices the energy density of full batteries [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Li C, Sun Y, Wu Q, Liang X, Chen C, Xiang H (2020) A novel design strategy of a practical carbon anode material from a single lignin-based surfactant source for sodium-ion batteries. Chem Commun 56(45):6078–6081" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e488">14</a>]. In the available reports, most of hard carbon anodes showed a reversible capacity of less than 200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Bu L, Kuai X, Zhu W, Huang X, Tian K, Lu H, Zhao J, Gao L (2020) Nitrogen-doped double-shell hollow carbon spheres for fast and stable sodium ion storage. Electrochim Acta 356:136804" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e494">15</a>]. Although some heteroatom-doped hard carbon demonstrated relatively high reversible capacity, a considerable portion of the capacity occurred at potentials above 1.5&nbsp;V as well as without obvious operating potential plateaus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="He L, Sun Y, Wang C, Guo H, Guo Y, Li C, Zhou Y (2020) High performance sulphur-doped pitch-based carbon materials as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. New Carbon Mater 35(4):420–427" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e497">16</a>], and the first coulombic efficiency was below 75%. In addition, complex preparation processes for some hard carbon with special morphology and microstructure greatly increased the cost of materials. In the future, it is very important to find hard carbon anode with high specific capacity, low operating potential, high first coulombic efficiency and low cost for the commercialization of sodium-ion batteries.</p><p>The use of biomass as raw material to prepare hard carbon has some unique advantages, such as diversity, low cost, low pollution and sustainability [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Sun Y, Shi XL, Yang YL, Suo G, Zhang L, Lu S, Chen ZG (2022) Biomass-derived carbon for high-performance batteries: from structure to properties. Adv Funct Mater 32(24):2201584" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e503">17</a>]. To date, some biochar has been developed to act as anodes for Na-ion batteries [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Song P, Wei S, Di J, Du J, Xu W, Liu D, Wang C, Qiao S, Cao Y, Cui Q, Zhang P, Ma L, Cui J, Wang Y, Xiong Y (2023) Biomass-derived hard carbon microtubes with tunable apertures for high-performance sodium-ion batteries. Nano Res 16:4874–4879" href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e506">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Aristote NT, Song Z, Deng W, Hou H, Zou G, Ji X (2023) Effect of double and triple-doping of sulfur, nitrogen and phosphorus on the initial coulombic efficiency and rate performance of the biomass derived hard carbon as anode for sodium-ion batteries. J Power Sources 558:232517" href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e506_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen Y, Li F, Guo Z, Song Z, Lin Y, Lin W, Zheng L, Huang Z, Hong Z, Titirici M (2023) Sustainable and scalable fabrication of high-performance hard carbon anode for Na-ion battery. J Power Sources 557:232534" href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e506_2">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim M, Fernando JFS, Li Z, Alowasheeir A, Ashok A, Xin R, Martin D, Kumar Nanjundan A, Golberg DV, Yamauchi Y, Amiralian N, Li J (2022) Ultra-stable sodium ion storage of biomass porous carbon derived from sugarcane. Chem Eng J 445:136344" href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e506_3">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hernández-Rentero C, Marangon V, Olivares-Marín M, Gómez-Serrano V, Caballero Á, Morales J, Hassoun J (2020) Alternative lithium-ion battery using biomass-derived carbons as environmentally sustainable anode. J Colloid Interface Sci 573:396–408" href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e506_4">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Senthil C, Park JW, Shaji N, Sim GS, Lee CW (2022) Biomass seaweed-derived nitrogen self-doped porous carbon anodes for sodium-ion batteries: insights into the structure and electrochemical activity. J Energy Chem 64:286–295" href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e506_5">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kumar DR, Kanagaraj I, Dhakal G, Prakash AS, Shim J (2021) Palmyra Palm tree biomass-derived carbon low-voltage plateau region capacity on Na-ion battery and its full cell performance. J Environ Chem Eng 9(4):105698" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e509">24</a>]. For instance, N-doped porous carbon with tailored surface area and pore structure was synthesized by using seaweed as carbon source, delivering a sodium storage capacity of 192 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Senthil C, Park JW, Shaji N, Sim GS, Lee CW (2022) Biomass seaweed-derived nitrogen self-doped porous carbon anodes for sodium-ion batteries: insights into the structure and electrochemical activity. J Energy Chem 64:286–295" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e517">23</a>]. Biomass carbon from the palmyra palm tree maintained 155 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> sodium storage capacity after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kumar DR, Kanagaraj I, Dhakal G, Prakash AS, Shim J (2021) Palmyra Palm tree biomass-derived carbon low-voltage plateau region capacity on Na-ion battery and its full cell performance. J Environ Chem Eng 9(4):105698" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e524">24</a>]. Uniform hard carbon microtubes (HCTs) were made from natural cotton through the one simple carbonization process [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Li Y, Hu Y, Titirici M, Chen L, Huang X (2016) Hard carbon microtubes made from renewable cotton as high-performance anode material for sodium-ion batteries. Adv Energy Mater 6(18):1600659" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e527">25</a>]. As an anode for Na-ion batteries, the HCTs carbonized at 1300&nbsp;°C delivered the highest reversible capacity of 315 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and good rate capability. Although some progress has been made, significant improvements in key indicators required for practical applications are still needed, such as specific capacity, operating potential, rate capability, cycle life and first coulombic efficiency.</p><p>Herein, a novel three-dimensional honeycomb-structured biochar (labelled as HS X-C) was reported. which was synthesized by milling and pyrolysis with the assistance of NaCl hard template, using Xanthium sibiricum fruits as raw material. Xanthium sibiricum usually grows in plains, hills and low mountains. Although it does not require manual management, the fruit yield is high. However, its economic value has not been well exploited. Wang [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Shi G, Han Z, Hu L, Wang B, Huang F (2022) N/O Co-doped hard carbon derived from cocklebur fruit for sodium-ion storage. ChemElectroChem 9(8):e202200138" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e535">26</a>] prepared hard carbon by direct pyrolysis of Xanthium sibiricum fruits and investigated the influence of pyrolysis temperature on its electrochemical performance. At 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the optimized hard carbon showed a reversible capacity of 252.88 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial coulombic efficiency of 69.08%. At 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the discharge capacity kept at 106.23 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles. In order to further improve the sodium storage performance of hard carbon from Xanthium sibiricum fruits, we conducted a series of studies to adjust its morphology and microstructure. It was found that the introduction of NaCl hard templates in the material preparation process easily changed the morphology and microstructure of the synthesized hard carbon, thereby significantly enhancing its electrochemical performance as anodes for Na-ion batteries. The final product showed a three-dimensional honeycomb-like structure (labelled as HS X-C). It exhibited a high initial coulombic efficiency of about 86%, an excellent rate capability of 161 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 4.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> and a long-term cyclability of 3000 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The assembled Na-ion full cells showed a high average operating voltage of about 3.3 V and a high energy density of about 180 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. In terms with specific capacity, rate capability and cycle life, the HS X-C anodes outperformed most of the previously reported biochar materials (Tab. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S1</a>). In addition, the kinetics of the HS X-C anodes in Na-ion batteries was also systematically investigated.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Experimental section"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Experimental section</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Material synthesis</h3><p>Scheme <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S1</a> shows the synthesis schematic diagram of the HS X-C. Xanthium sibiricum fruits were taken from nature. After washing and drying, they were ground into powder with a grinder. Then, 2 g powder and 8 g NaCl were added into a steel tank with a ball-to-material ratio of 15:1 and milled on a planetary mill at 550 rpm for 6 h; subsequently, the high-temperature treatment was divided into two stages. First, the milled mixture was transferred to a corundum boat and annealed at 600 °C for 2 h in an argon atmosphere. After natural cooling, the solid was washed with distilled water to remove NaCl. Second, the collected product was dried at 110 °C in vacuum for 8 h and then further annealed in argon at 1000 °C for 2 h. The final product was labelled as HS X-C. For comparison, the hard carbon from Xanthium sibiricum fruits without the assistance of NaCl template was prepared through directly annealing the milled Xanthium sibiricum fruits at 1000 °C for 2 h, which was labelled as X-C. During annealing treatments, heating rate and argon flow used were 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup> and 80 mL min<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Material characterization</h3><p>The morphology, microstructure and composition of the synthesized products were characterized using scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Gemini-500), transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL 2100F) and energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS, Oxford max150). The specific surface area measurement was performed on a Micromeritics 2460 tester at 77 K, during which N<sub>2</sub> was used as the adsorption/desorption gas. The graphitization degree of carbon was investigated by an X-ray diffractometer (XRD, D8 Discover) and a Raman spectrometer (Witec alpha300). The chemical states and contents of the elements were detected by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (Escalab 250xi) and elemental analysis (EA, elementar vario EL cube).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Electrochemical measurement</h3><p>The Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties of the as-synthesized hard carbon were measured by the assembled 2032 cells. The working electrode was composed of the tested material, Super P carbon black and sodium alginate in a weight ratio of 75:10:15, in which copper foil served as the current collector and the active material loading was about 1.0&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. In a half cell, the counter electrode was Na metal disc. In a full cell, the cathode was composed of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/rGO, the preparation of which was referred to our previous work [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cai S, Yan F, Zhao Y, Li M, Chen Y, He X, Wang C (2022) Hierarchical micro-composite assembled from Bi spheres and expanded graphite flakes as anodes for sodium-ion half/full cells with excellent comprehensive electrochemical performance. Chem Eng J 430:132938" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e608">27</a>], Super P carbon black and polyvinylidene fluoride in a weight of 83:10:7. The positive and negative capacity ratio is 1:1.2. The electrolyte was 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme, while the separator was glass fibre (Whatman GF/A). Galvanostatic charge/discharge and galvanostatic intermittent titration (GITT) measurements were performed on a Neware battery tester. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectrum (EIS) measurements were performed on an EC-lab electrochemical workstation. The amplitude and frequency range for EIS were 10&nbsp;mV and 100&nbsp;kHz–100&nbsp;mHz, respectively.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>The SEM images of the X-C synthesized from directly pyrolyzing ground Xanthium sibiricum fruits are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S1</a>. It was irregular solid blocks with obvious agglomeration characteristics. However, with the introduction of NaCl hard template, the synthesized HS X-C showed significant difference in morphology and microstructure.</p><p>From Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig1">1</a>a and b, it delivered a three-dimensional honeycomb-like structure with a wall thickness of less than 20 nm. The TEM image in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig1">1</a>c further confirmed this kind of special structure. HRTEM image revealed that the HS X-C was long-range-disordered but short-range-ordered in terms of crystalline property (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig1">1</a>d). The (002) lattice spacing was around 0.37 nm, which was larger than 0.335 nm for the graphite [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zheng T, Zhong X, Li M, Yan F, Cai S, Zhang W, Liu L, Wang D (2021) Fabrication of Bi nanospheres assembled microspheres with petal structure || B, N-co-doped C nanosheets hybrid sodium-ion capacitors with ultrahigh power density, high energy density and long cycle life. J Power Sources 515:230638" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e637">28</a>]. The discontinuous lattice strips should be attributed to the abundance of heteroatoms in biomass raw materials. During the heat treatment, some non-metallic heteroatoms could be doped into C, while the others were released in gas form. In addition, the etching of metal ions could also lead to discontinuity of lattice strips. Elemental analysis showed that the N and O heteroatom contents were about 1.1% and 5.2%, respectively, but no S element was detected. From the hierarchical elemental distribution map in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S2</a>, the doped N and O elements were distributed evenly. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig1">1</a>e shows the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of the HS X-C. When the relative pressure (<i>p</i>/<i>p</i><sub><i>0</i></sub>) increased from 0 to 0.4, the amount of adsorbed N<sub>2</sub> reached 105 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that there were many micropores. During the increase of <i>p</i>/<i>p</i><sub><i>0</i></sub> from 0.4 to 0.8, the amount of adsorbed N<sub>2</sub> increased only by 64 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. Considering that no obvious hysteresis ring appeared between 0.4 and 0.8, the number of middle pores was relatively small. After <i>p</i>/<i>p</i><sub><i>0</i></sub> exceeded 0.8, the amount of adsorbed N<sub>2</sub> increased rapidly with increasing pressure, indicative of the existence of many macropores [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Groen JC, Peffer LAA, Pérez-Ramı́rez J (2003) Pore size determination in modified micro and mesoporous materials. Pitfalls and limitations in gas adsorption data analysis. Microporous Mesoporous Mat 60(1–3):1–17" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e693">29</a>]. These characteristics were well accorded with the pore size distribution curve in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig1">1</a>f. Calculated on the desorption isothermal curve, the BET surface area and total pore volume of the HS X-C were about 299 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and 0.91 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The high specific surface area and abundant voids contributed to improving the contact area between material and electrolyte, facilitating the penetration of electrolyte, promoting the transportation of ions and easing the relaxation of the tension, so the HS X-C was expected to exhibit high capacity and excellent rate performance.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11581-023-05235-0/MediaObjects/11581_2023_5235_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11581-023-05235-0/MediaObjects/11581_2023_5235_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="725"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <b>b</b> SEM images, <b>c</b> TEM and (<b>d)</b> HRTEM images, <b>e</b> N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms and (<b>f)</b> pore size distribution curve of the HS X-C</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The XRD pattern and Raman spectrum of the HS X-C are shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig2">2</a>a and b. Although no sharp peak was observed in the XRD pattern, two obvious broad peaks appeared at 23° and 43°, which were assigned to (002) and (100) planes of graphite respectively. Such a situation was consistent with a short-range-ordered structure.&nbsp;In the Raman spectrum, two peaks were assigned to the vibration of disordered carbon (D-band) and graphitized carbon (G-band) were located at 1350 and 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The fitting analysis revealed that the peak intensity ratio (I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>) was about 0.85, further confirming that the HS X-C had a relatively high graphitization. XPS was used to detect the chemical composition of the surface. As shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig2">2</a>c, the chemical composition was relatively simple, where only C, N and O elements were detected. The C 1s HRXPS spectrum could be fitted into four peaks at 284.4, 285, 285.9 and 287.4 eV, corresponding to C–C, C-N, C-O and C = O bonds, respectively (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig2">2</a>d) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Wang X, Du J, Zhang Q, Gu L, Cao L, Liang H (2020) In situ synthesis of sustainable highly efficient single iron atoms anchored on nitrogen doped carbon derived from renewable biomass. Carbon 157:614–621" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e765">30</a>]. The N 1s HRXPS spectrum could be deconvoluted into two peaks at 398 and 400 eV (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig2">2</a>e), which belonged to pyridinic N and pyrrole N, respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Senthil C, Park JW, Shaji N, Sim GS, Lee CW (2022) Biomass seaweed-derived nitrogen self-doped porous carbon anodes for sodium-ion batteries: insights into the structure and electrochemical activity. J Energy Chem 64:286–295" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e771">23</a>]. The O 1s HRXPS spectrum could be divided into three peaks at 531.1, 532.2 and 533.2 eV, which corresponded to C = O, C-O and C–O–C bonds, respectively (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig2">2</a>f) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Muruganantham R, Wang F, Liu W (2022) A green route N, S-doped hard carbon derived from fruit-peel biomass waste as an anode material for rechargeable sodium-ion storage applications. Electrochim Acta 424:140573" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e777">31</a>]. These results showed that both N and O were doped into C host. Compared with C atom, since N and O had redundant free electrons and higher electronegativity, it was easy to cause electric field imbalance, thereby providing more adsorption sites for alkali metal ions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wang H, Chen J, Wang P, Liang C, Yu K (2022) Preparation and mechanism of biomass-derived graphene-like Li+/Na+ battery anodes controlled by N/O functional groups and interlayer spacing. J Alloys Compd 918:165785" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e781">32</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11581-023-05235-0/MediaObjects/11581_2023_5235_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11581-023-05235-0/MediaObjects/11581_2023_5235_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="352"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> XRD pattern, <b>b</b> Raman spectrum, <b>c</b> XPS survey, <b>d</b> C 1s HRXPS, <b>e</b> N 1s HRXPS and (<b>f)</b> O 1s HRXPS spectra of the HS X-C</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The electrochemical behaviour of the HS X-C as an anode of Na-ion batteries was investigated. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig3">3</a>a shows its initial three CV curves at 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. A pair of sharp redox peaks presented at approximately 0.044 and 0.095 V with a peak potential difference of 51 mV. The other peaks were very weak and broad. In addition, it was interesting that even in the first cathodic scan, no distinct peak of electrolyte decomposition was observed, implying that the anode could possess a high first coulombic efficiency. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig3">3</a>b shows the charge–discharge profiles of different cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The discharge profiles could be roughly divided into three characteristic regions. In this region of higher than about 1.15 V, the discharge profile was steep, corresponding to the process of Na<sup>+</sup> deposition in the defect area of the material. In the region of less than 0.07 V, the discharge profile was flat, corresponding to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the “pseudo-graphite” layer [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yan L, Wang J, Ren Q, Fan L, Liu B, Zhang L, He L, Mei X, Shi Z (2022) In-situ graphene-coated carbon microsphere as high initial coulombic efficiency anode for superior Na/K-ion full cell. Chem Eng J 432:133257" href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e837">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bommier C, Surta TW, Dolgos M, Ji X (2015) New mechanistic insights on Na-ion storage in nongraphitizable carbon. Nano Lett 15(9):5888–5892" href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e837_1">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Ren Q, Wang J, Yan L, Lv W, Zhang F, Zhang L, Liu B, Shi Z (2021) Manipulating free-standing, flexible and scalable microfiber carbon papers unlocking ultra-high initial Coulombic efficiency and storage sodium behavior. Chem Eng J 425:131656" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e840">35</a>]. The intermediate region at 1.0–0.07 V corresponded to the process of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into disordered carbon [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Anji Reddy M, Helen M, Groß A, Fichtner M, Euchner H (2018) Insight into sodium insertion and the storage mechanism in hard carbon. ACS Energy Lett 3(12):2851–2857" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e845">36</a>]. The three regional characteristics in the charge profiles were also obvious. The first discharge and charge capacities were 270.5 and 234 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with a high first coulombic efficiency of 86.5%. Generally, the anode material with high specific surface area delivered a low first coulombic efficiency because extra electrolyte was consumed for the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film. However, in this work, in addition to no obvious electrolyte decomposition peaks in the first CV cathodic scan, the clear honeycomb structure of the material could still be observed even after 100 cycles (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S3</a> (a-c)), indicative of a thin SEI layer. To confirm that the high first coulombic efficiency resulted from the solvent of the electrolyte, ester solvent (EC:DMC:EMC = 1:1:1 Vol%, 5.0% FEC) was used to replace diglyme and the results are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S3</a> (d-g). From Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S3</a> (d, e), significant differences were observed on the surface of the electrode, where the honeycomb structure of the HS X-C could not be observed due to the covering of a thick SEI film. From Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S3</a> (c, f), in the first CV curve, the electrode cycled in the electrolyte using diglyme as solvent had only negligible reduction peak at 0.74 V, while that cycled in the electrolyte with ester as solvent presented three strong reduction peaks at 0.35, 0.78 and 1.0 V. Moreover, the first coulombic efficiency sharply decreased to 28% (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S3</a> (g)). Therefore, it could be concluded that diglyme was less prone to reductive decomposition on the surface of the HS X-C. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig3">3</a>c shows the cycle performance of the HS X-C at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In the 200 cycles, although the capacity decreased slowly in the first 8 cycles, it had maintained at about 202 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the subsequent cycles. When cycling at different current densities from 0.1, 0.5, 1, 2 and 4 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the stable capacities were 235, 216, 206, 191 and 161 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig3">3</a>d), indicating that the anode possessed superior rate capability. From Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig3">3</a>e, the anode also had long cycle stability at a high current density. After 3000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a discharge capacity of 185 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was kept, with capacity retention of 96.9% against the stable capacity of 191 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. According to Tab. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S1</a>, compared with previously reported biochar anodes for Na-ion batteries, the HS X-C overall exhibited higher specific capacity, better rate capability and more excellent cycle stability.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11581-023-05235-0/MediaObjects/11581_2023_5235_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig3" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11581-023-05235-0/MediaObjects/11581_2023_5235_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure 3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="500"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Electrochemical behaviours and microstructure change of the HS X-C electrodes in Na-ion half cells: <b>a</b> CV curves in the first three cycles at 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, <b>b</b> charge/discharge profiles in different cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, <b>c</b> cycle performance at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, <b>d</b> rate capability at 0.1, 0.5, 1, 2 and 4 A g<sup>−1</sup>, <b>e</b> long cycle stability at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, <b>f</b> EIS after different cycles and (<b>g)</b>, <b>h</b> TEM images after 1000 cycles</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To understand the reasons for the improvement of electrochemical performance of the HS X-C, in addition to investigating the impedance and structural changes of the electrodes during cycling, the structural influence of the synthesized carbon was also compared. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig3">3</a>f shows the variation of electrochemical impedance with cycle number. All EIS consisted of two semicircles in the medium and high frequency area and a slope line in the low frequency area. In the equivalent circuit diagram, Rs represented the solution resistance, R<sub>SEI</sub> denoted the resistance associated with the migration of Na-ion through the solid electrolyte interface and Rct was the charge transfer resistance during the electrochemical reaction [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ou J, Wang J, Zhao G, Zou L, Lei Y (2020) Buckwheat derived nitrogen-rich porous carbon material with a high-performance Na-storage. J Porous Mat 27(4):1139–1147" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e957">37</a>]. The fitting results was shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S2</a>. The Rs and R<sub>SEI</sub> values after 200 cycles showed only a negligible increase when compared with that after 50 cycles, indicative of high structural stability of the electrode. In addition, the Rct values decreased with the cycle, indicating that the charge transfer speed and the electrochemical reaction rate increased. From the TEM images of the cycled HS X-C electrode in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig3">3</a>g, h, the honeycomb structure of the material was well maintained after 1000 cycles, indicating that such a structure could indeed accommodate well to the tension generated by volume changes. Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#MOESM1">S4</a> shows the cycle performance of the X-C at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the discharge capacity of which had continuously been declining. After 450 cycles, the discharge capacity decreased to 106 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with a low-capacity retention of 44.7% compared with the second discharge capacity. Evidently, introducing NaCl template during the synthesis of material significantly improved the cyclic stability of carbon from Xanthium sibiricum fruits. The excellent electrochemical performance of the HS X-C should be attributed to its unique microstructure, which not only reduced the transmission path of electrons and ions, but also facilitated the release of tension.</p><p>To study the kinetics of the electrode process, CV curves were recorded at different scan rates from 0.2 to 1.4 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig4">4</a>a, as scan rate increased, all peaks increased in intensity but there was little change in peak potentials, indicating that the electrochemical reaction was highly reversible. The control step of the electrode process was determined by <i>I</i><sub>p</sub> = aν<sup>b</sup>, where b = 0.5 and 1 represent diffusion control and surface adsorption/reaction control, respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Li D, Zhang Y, Sun Q, Zhang S, Wang Z, Liang Z, Si P, Ci L (2019) Hierarchically porous carbon supported Sn4P3 as a superior anode material for potassium-ion batteries. Energy Storage Mater 23:367–374" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e990">38</a>].&nbsp;Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig4">4</a>b presents the relation curve of log<i>I</i><sub><i>p</i></sub><i> –</i> log<i>ν</i> of redox peaks, where b<sub>R</sub> and b<sub>O</sub> were 0.85 and 0.75, respectively, indicating that the reaction rate was jointly controlled by diffusion and capacitance. The capacitive contribution was further calculated by<i> I</i> = k<sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + k<sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, where k<sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and k<sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represent the contribution of the surface-driven pseudocapacitive process and the contribution of the diffusion-controlled reaction, respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ou H, Huang J, Zhou Y, Zhu J, Fang G, Cao X, Li J, Liang S (2022) Surface-dominated ultra-stable sodium and potassium storage enabled by N/P/O tri-doped porous carbon. Chem Eng J 450:138444" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e1035">39</a>]. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig4">4</a>c shows the variation of the capacitance contribution with the scanning rate. As the scanning rate increased from 0.2 to 1.4 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the capacitance contribution increased from 73 to 86%. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig4">4</a>d shows the capacitance contribution corresponding to different potentials at 0.8 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The capacitance contribution between 0.6 and 3 V was significantly greater than that between 0 and 0.2 V, indicating that the electrode process was more controlled by diffusion during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction into/out the pseudo-graphite layer. The diffusion coefficient (D) of Na<sup>+</sup> was measured using the GITT technique. The recorded GITT curves was shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig4">4</a>e. D was calculated on the formula as follows:</p><div id="Equa" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$D=4/\pi \tau {\left(\frac{{m}_{B}{V}_{M}}{{M}_{B}S}\right)}^{2}{\left(\frac{{\Delta E}_{S}}{{\Delta E}_{\tau }}\right)}^{2}$$</span></div></div><p>where <i>τ</i> is the duration of the pulse current, and <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> is the weight of active material, <i>S</i> is the total area of the electrode, Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> is the voltage change during rest, Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> is the voltage change during pulse, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> is the molar volume of the active material and <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> is the molar mass of the active material [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Tao L, Yang Y, Wang H, Zheng Y, Hao H, Song W, Shi J, Huang M, Mitlin D (2020) Sulfur-nitrogen rich carbon as stable high capacity potassium ion battery anode: Performance and storage mechanisms. Energy Storage Mater 27:212–225" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e1199">40</a>]. In this work, each pulse was composed of 10&nbsp;min of titration with a current density of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 30&nbsp;min of rest. To reflect the speed of ion diffusion more accurately, the actual surface area of the active material was used to replace the apparent area of the electrode. The relative value of M<sub>B</sub>/V<sub>M</sub> was calculated based on the following formulation:</p><div id="Equb" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\rho =\frac{1}{{\mathrm{V}}_{pore}+\frac{1}{{\rho }_{carbon}}}$$</span></div></div><p>where <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\rho\)</span> (g cm<sup>−3</sup>) is the density of materials, <i>V</i><sub><i>pore</i></sub> (cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) is the total pore volume analyzed from the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm, and <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\rho }_{carbon}\)</span> is the true density of carbon (2 g cm<sup>−3</sup>). The calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients for discharge and charge are also shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig4">4</a>e. The <i>D</i> values were between 3.8 × 10<sup>−15</sup> ~ 8.2 × 10<sup>−17</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> during discharging, while the <i>D</i> values were between 5.0 × 10<sup>−15</sup> ~ 1.3 × 10<sup>−17</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> during charging. During discharging, the ion diffusion rate presented a rapid increase between 1.2 and 0.6 V and then gradually decreased until the cutoff voltage, indicating that the micropores were more accessible than the interlayer space in the hard carbon. The rapid decline of ion diffusion rate in the plateau region was because Na<sup>+</sup> had to overcome a charge repulsion from the Na<sup>+</sup> bound in micropores to insert interlayer space [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Groen JC, Peffer LAA, Pérez-Ramı́rez J (2003) Pore size determination in modified micro and mesoporous materials. Pitfalls and limitations in gas adsorption data analysis. Microporous Mesoporous Mat 60(1–3):1–17" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e1363">29</a>]. During charging, the detachment of Na<sup>+</sup> from micropores was relatively easy, where a sudden increase in ion diffusion rate was also observed.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11581-023-05235-0/MediaObjects/11581_2023_5235_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig4" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11581-023-05235-0/MediaObjects/11581_2023_5235_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure 4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="504"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Dynamics of the HS X-C anodes in Na-ion half cells: <b>a</b> CV curves at various scan rates from 0.2 to 1.4 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, <b>b</b> fitting curves of log<i>I</i>—log <i>v</i>, <b>c</b> capacitance contribution at various scan rates, <b>d</b> capacitance contribution of different potential at 0.8 mV s<sup>−1</sup> and (<b>e)</b> GITT curves. Electrochemical performance of HS X-C||NVP/rGO Na-ion full cell: <b>f</b> charge/discharge profiles, <b>g</b> cycle performance at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup><sub>(cathode)</sub> and (<b>h)</b> rate performance at 0.1, 0.2, 0.4 and 0.6 A g.<sup>−1</sup><sub>(cathode)</sub></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Full cells were assembled to further evaluate the feasibility of the HS X-C anode, in which the cathode used was NVP/rGO previously reported by our group [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cai S, Yan F, Zhao Y, Li M, Chen Y, He X, Wang C (2022) Hierarchical micro-composite assembled from Bi spheres and expanded graphite flakes as anodes for sodium-ion half/full cells with excellent comprehensive electrochemical performance. Chem Eng J 430:132938" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1265338e1430">27</a>]. Herein, the mentioned current density and specific capacity were calculated on the weight of NVP/rGO. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig4">4</a>f and g show the charge/discharge profiles and cycle performance at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup><sub>(cathode)</sub>. The charge/discharge profiles displayed a long voltage platform, low polarization and high operating potential. The charge potential plateau reached 3.4 V, ensuring high energy density of full cells. As shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig4">4</a>g, the full cell had good cycle stability. Based on the total weight of the anode and the cathode as well as an average operating voltage of 3.3 V, it could be calculated that the full cell delivered a high energy density of about 180 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup><sub>(total)</sub>. The rate performance is shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11581-023-05235-0#Fig4">4</a>h. When cycling at different current densities from 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6 and 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the stable capacities were 97, 95, 88, 79 and 95 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. These results indicated that the HS X-C was a potential anode for high-performance sodium-ion batteries.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>A novel three-dimensional honeycomb-like N, O-doped biochar was successfully synthesized from Xanthium sibiricum by simple milling and pyrolysis with the help of NaCl template. Thanks to unique structure, high specific surface area and abundant defects, the HS X-C exhibited high capacity, excellent rate capability and long cycle stability. The high first coulombic efficiency was because diglyme was less prone to reductive decomposition on the surface of the HS X-C. The Na-ion full cells paired with the NVP/rGO cathode showed a high average operating voltage of 3.3 V and a high energy density of 180 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup><sub>(total)</sub>. The dynamics of the HS X-C anode was jointly controlled by diffusion and pseudocapacitance. This work not only significantly improved the sodium storage performance of biochar from Xanthium sibiricum fruits, but also demonstrated the importance of the synthesis methods of biochar, providing a new perspective for fully utilizing biochar.</p></div></div></section>
                                </div>
